Introduction
These are the lecture notes for a short course entitled â€œIntroduction to Lie groups and
symplectic geometryâ€ which I gave at the 1991 Regional Geometry Institute at Park City,
Utah starting on 24 June and ending on 11 July.
The course really was designed to be an introduction, aimed at an audience of stu-
dents who were familiar with basic constructions in diï¬€erential topology and rudimentary
diï¬€erential geometry, who wanted to get a feel for Lie groups and symplectic geometry.
My purpose was not to provide an exhaustive treatment of either Lie groups, which would
have been impossible even if I had had an entire year, or of symplectic manifolds, which
has lately undergone something of a revolution. Instead, I tried to provide an introduction
to what I regard as the basic concepts of the two subjects, with an emphasis on examples
which drove the development of the theory.
I deliberately tried to include a few topics which are not part of the mainstream
subject, such as Lieâ€™s reduction of order for diï¬€erential equations and its relation with
the notion of a solvable group on the one hand and integration of ODE by quadrature on
the other. I also tried, in the later lectures to introduce the reader to some of the global
methods which are now becoming so important in symplectic geometry. However, a full
treatment of these topics in the space of nine lectures beginning at the elementary level
was beyond my abilities.
After the lectures were over, I contemplated reworking these notes into a comprehen-
sive introduction to modern symplectic geometry and, after some soul-searching, ï¬nally
decided against this. Thus, I have contented myself with making only minor modiï¬cations
and corrections, with the hope that an interested person could read these notes in a few
weeks and get some sense of what the subject was about.
An essential feature of the course was the exercise sets. Each set begins with elemen-
tary material and works up to more involved and delicate problems. My object was to
provide a path to understanding of the material which could be entered at several diï¬€erent
levels and so the exercises vary greatly in diï¬ƒculty. Many of these exercise sets are obvi-
ously too long for any person to do them during the three weeks the course, so I provided
extensive hints to aid the student in completing the exercises after the course was over.
I want to take this opportunity to thank the many people who made helpful sugges-
tions for these notes both during and after the course. Particular thanks goes to Karen
Uhlenbeck and Dan Freed, who invited me to give an introductory set of lectures at the
RGI, and to my course assistant, Tom Ivey, who provided invaluable help and criticism in
the early stages of the notes and tirelessly helped the students with the exercises. While
the faults of the presentation are entirely my own, without the help, encouragement, and
proofreading contributed by these folks and others, neither these notes nor the course
would never have come to pass.

Background Material and Basic Terminology. In these lectures, I assume that
the reader is familiar with the basic notions of manifolds, vector ï¬elds, and diï¬€erential
forms. All manifolds will be assumed to be both second countable and Hausdorï¬€. Also,
unless I say otherwise, I generally assume that all maps and manifolds are Câˆ.
Since it came up several times in the course of the course of the lectures, it is probably
worth emphasizing the following point: A submanifold of a smooth manifold X is, by
deï¬nition, a pair (S, f) where S is a smooth manifold and f: S â†’X is a one-to-one
immersion. In particular, f need not be an embedding.
The notation I use for smooth manifolds and mappings is fairly standard, but with a
few slight variations:
If f: X â†’Y is a smooth mapping, then fâ€²: TX â†’TY denotes the induced mapping
on tangent bundles, with fâ€²(x) denoting its restriction to TxX. (However, I follow tradition
when X = R and let fâ€²(t) stand for fâ€²(t)(âˆ‚/âˆ‚t) for all t âˆˆR. I trust that this abuse of
notation will not cause confusion.)
For any vector space V , I generally use Ap(V ) (instead of, say, Î›p(V âˆ—)) to denote
the space of alternating (or exterior) p-forms on V . For a smooth manifold M, I denote
the space of smooth, alternating p-forms on M by Ap(M). The algebra of all (smooth)
diï¬€erential forms on M is denoted by Aâˆ—(M).
I generally reserve the letter d for the exterior derivative d: Ap(M) â†’Ap+1(M).
For any vector ï¬eld X on M, I will denote left-hook with X (often called interior

Jets. Occasionally, it will be convenient to use the language of jets in describing
certain constructions. Jets provide a coordinate free way to talk about the Taylor expansion
of some mapping up to a speciï¬ed order. No detailed knowledge about these objects will
be needed in these lectures, so the following comments should suï¬ƒce:
If f and g are two smooth maps from a manifold Xm to a manifold Y n, we say that
f and g agree to order k at x âˆˆX if, ï¬rst, f(x) = g(x) = y âˆˆY and, second, when
u: U â†’Rm and v: V â†’Rn are local coordinate systems centered on x and y respectively,
the functions F = v â—¦f â—¦uâˆ’1 and G = v â—¦g â—¦uâˆ’1 have the same Taylor series at 0 âˆˆRm up
to and including order k. Using the Chain Rule, it is not hard to show that this condition
is independent of the choice of local coordinates u and v centered at x and y respectively.
The notation f â‰¡x,k g will mean that f and g agree to order k at x. This is easily
seen to deï¬ne an equivalence relation. Denote the â‰¡x,k-equivalence class of f by jk(f)(x),
and call it the k-jet of f at x.
For example, knowing the 1-jet at x of a map f: X â†’Y is equivalent to knowing both
f(x) and the linear map fâ€²(x): Tx â†’Tf(x)Y .

The set of k-jets of maps from X to Y is usually denoted by J k(X, Y ). It is not hard
to show that J k(X, Y ) can be given a unique smooth manifold structure in such a way
that, for any smooth f: X â†’Y , the obvious map jk(f): X â†’J k(X, Y ) is also smooth.
These jet spaces have various functorial properties which we shall not need at all.
The main reason for introducing this notion is to give meaning to concise statements like
â€œThe critical points of f are determined by its 1-jetâ€, â€œThe curvature at x of a Riemannian
metric g is determined by its 2-jet at xâ€, or, from Lecture 8, â€œThe integrability of an almost
complex structure J: TX â†’TX is determined by its 1-jetâ€. Should the reader wish to
learn more about jets, I recommend the ï¬rst two chapters of [GG].

The relationship between symmetry and solvability in diï¬€erential equations is pro-
found and far reaching. The subjects which are now known as Lie groups and symplectic
geometry got their beginnings from the study of symmetries of systems of ordinary diï¬€er-
ential equations and of integration techniques for them.
By the middle of the nineteenth century, Galois theory had clariï¬ed the relationship
between the solvability of polynomial equations by radicals and the group of â€œsymmetriesâ€
of the equations. Sophus Lie set out to do the same thing for diï¬€erential equations and
their symmetries.
Here is a â€œdictionaryâ€ showing the (rough) correspondence which Lie developed be-
tween these two achievements of nineteenth century mathematics.

Although the full explanation of these correspondances must await the later lectures, we
can at least begin the story in the simplest examples as motivation for developing the
general theory. This is what I shall do for the rest of todayâ€™s lecture.

It is going to turn out that, just as in the case of the Riccati equation, the prescribed
curvature and torsion equations cannot be solved by algebraic manipulations and quadra-

of the line which ï¬x the cross-ratio of four points.

sions, which are symmetries of the ODE because they preserve V . That is, V (R(x)) = V (x)
whenever R is a linear transformation satisfying RtR = I. The equation RtR = I describes
a locus in the space of 3 Ã— 3 matrices. Later on we will see this locus is a smooth compact
3-manifold, which is also a group, called O(3). The group of rotations, and generalizations
thereof, will play a central role in subsequent lectures.

Lecture 2:
Lie Groups and Lie Algebras
Lie Groups. In this lecture, I deï¬ne and develop some of the basic properties of the
central objects of interest in these lectures: Lie groups and Lie algebras.
Deï¬nition 1: A Lie group is a pair (G, Âµ) where G is a smooth manifold and Âµ: GÃ—G â†’G
is a smooth mapping which gives G the structure of a group.
When the multiplication Âµ is clear from context, we usually just say â€œG is a Lie group.â€
Also, for the sake of notational sanity, I will follow the practice of writing Âµ(a, b) simply as
ab whenever this will not cause confusion. I will usually denote the multiplicative identity
by e âˆˆG and the multiplicative inverse of a âˆˆG by aâˆ’1 âˆˆG.
Most of the algebraic constructions in the theory of abstract groups have straightfor-
ward analogues for Lie groups:
Deï¬nition 2:
A Lie subgroup of a Lie group G is a subgroup H âŠ‚G which is also a
submanifold of G. A Lie group homomorphism is a group homomorphism Ï†: H â†’G which
is also a smooth mapping of the underlying manifolds.
Here is the prototypical example of a Lie group:
Example :
The General Linear Group. The (real) general linear group in dimen-
sion n, denoted GL(n, R), is the set of invertible n-by-n real matrices regarded as an open
submanifold of the n2-dimensional vector space of all n-by-n real matrices with multipli-
cation map Âµ given by matrix multiplication: Âµ(a, b) = ab. Since the matrix product ab is
deï¬ned by a formula which is polynomial in the matrix entries of a and b, it is clear that
GL(n, R) is a Lie group.
Actually, if V is any ï¬nite dimensional real vector space, then GL(V ), the set of
bijective linear maps Ï†: V â†’V , is an open subset of the vector space End(V ) = V âŠ—V âˆ—and
becomes a Lie group when endowed with the multiplication Âµ: GL(V ) Ã— GL(V ) â†’GL(V )
given by composition of maps: Âµ(Ï†1, Ï†2) = Ï†1 â—¦Ï†2.
If dim(V ) = n, then GL(V ) is
isomorphic (as a Lie group) to GL(n, R), though not canonically.
The advantage of considering abstract vector spaces V rather than just Rn is mainly
conceptual, but, as we shall see, this conceptual advantage is great. In fact, Lie groups of
linear transformations are so fundamental that a special terminology is reserved for them:
Deï¬nition 3: A (linear) representation of a Lie group G is a Lie group homomorphism
Ï: G â†’GL(V ) for some vector space V called the representation space. Such a representa-
tion is said to be faithful (resp., almost faithful ) if Ï is one-to-one (resp., has 0-dimensional
kernel).
L.2.1
12

It is a consequence of a theorem of Ado and Iwasawa that every connected Lie group
has an almost faithful, ï¬nite-dimensional representation. (In one of the later exercises, we
will construct a connected Lie group which has no faithful, ï¬nite-dimensional representa-
tion, so almost faithful is the best we can hope for.)
Example: Vector Spaces. Any vector space over R becomes a Lie group when the
group â€œmultiplicationâ€ is taken to be addition.
Example: Matrix Lie Groups. The Lie subgroups of GL(n, R) are called matrix Lie
groups and play an important role in the theory. Not only are they the most frequently
encountered, but, because of the theorem of Ado and Iwasawa, practically anything which
is true for matrix Lie groups has an analog for a general Lie group. In fact, for the ï¬rst pass
through, the reader can simply imagine that all of the Lie groups mentioned are matrix
Lie groups. Here are a few simple examples:
1. Let An be the set of diagonal n-by-n matrices with positive entries on the diagonal.
2. Let Nn be the set of upper triangular n-by-n matrices with all diagonal entries all
equal to 1.
3. (n = 2 only) Let Câ€¢ =

special orthogonal group in dimension n. In each case, one must check that the given
subset is actually a subgroup and submanifold of GL(n, R). These are exercises for the
reader. (See the problems at the end of this lecture for hints.)
A Lie group can have â€œwildâ€ subgroups which cannot be given the structure of a Lie
group. For example, (R, +) is a Lie group which contains totally disconnected, uncountable
subgroups. Since all of our manifolds are second countable, such subgroups (by deï¬nition)
cannot be given the structure of a (0-dimensional) Lie group.
However, it can be shown [Wa, pg. 110] that any closed subgroup of a Lie group G is
an embedded submanifold of G and hence is a Lie subgroup. However, for reasons which
will soon become apparent, it is disadvantageous to consider only closed subgroups.

â–¡
As our last general Theorem, we state, without proof, the following existence result.
Theorem 4: For each ï¬nite dimensional Lie algebra g, there exists a Lie group G whose
Lie algebra is isomorphic to g.
Unfortunately, this theorem is surprisingly diï¬ƒcult to prove.
It would suï¬ƒce, by
Theorem 2, to show that every Lie algebra g is isomorphic to a subalgebra of the Lie
algebra of a Lie group. In fact, an even stronger statement is true. A theorem of Ado
asserts that every ï¬nite dimensional Lie algebra is isomorphic to a subalgebra of gl(n, R)
for some n.
Thus, to prove Theorem 4, it would be enough to prove Adoâ€™s theorem.
Unfortunately, this theorem also turns out to be rather delicate (see [Po] for a proof).
However, there are many interesting examples of g for which a proof can be given by
elementary means (see the Exercises).
On the other hand, this abstract existence theorem is not used very often anyway.
It is rare that a (ï¬nite dimensional) Lie algebra arises in practice which is not readily
representable as the Lie algebra of some Lie group.
The reader may be wondering about uniqueness: How many Lie groups are there
whose Lie algebras are isomorphic to a given g? Since the Lie algebra of a Lie group
G only depends on the identity component G, it is reasonable to restrict to the case of
connected Lie groups. Now, as you are asked to show in the Exercises, the universal cover
ËœG of a connected Lie group G can be given a unique Lie group structure for which the
covering map ËœG â†’G is a homomorphism. Thus, there always exists a connected and
simply connected Lie group, say G(g), whose Lie algebra is isomorphic to g. A simple
L.2.15
26

application of Theorem 3 shows that if Gâ€² is any other Lie group with Lie algebra g, then
there is a homomorphism Ï†: G(g) â†’G which induces an isomorphism on the Lie algebras.
It follows easily that, up to isomorphism, there is only one simply connected and connected
Lie group with Lie algebra g. Moreover, every other connected Lie group with Lie algebra
G is isomorphic to a quotient of G(g) by a discrete subgroup of G which lies in the center
of G(g) (see the Exercises).
The Structure Constants. Our work so far has shown that the problem of classify-
ing the connected Lie groups up to isomorphism is very nearly the same thing as classifying
the (ï¬nite dimensional) Lie algebras. (See the Exercises for a clariï¬cation of this point.)
This is a remarkable state of aï¬€airs, since, a priori, Lie groups involve the topology of
smooth manifolds and it is rather surprising that their classiï¬cation can be reduced to
what is essentially an algebra problem. It is worth taking a closer look at this algebra
problem itself.
Let g be a Lie algebra of dimension n, and let x1, x2, . . . , xn be a basis for g. Then
there exist constants ck
ij so that (using the summation convention)
[xi, xj] = ck
ij xk .
(These quantities c are called the structure constants of g relative to the given basis.) The
skew-symmetry of the Lie bracket is is equivalent to the skew-symmetry of c in its lower
indices:
ck

ji = 0.
The Jacobi identity is equivalent to the quadratic equations:

ij cm

iâ„“+ câ„“
ki cm
jâ„“= 0.
Conversely, any set of n3 constants satisfying these relations deï¬nes an n-dimensional Lie
algebra by the above bracket formula.

An Extended Example: 2- and 3-dimensional Lie Algebras. It is clear that
up to isomorphism, there is only one (real) Lie algebra of dimension 1, namely g = R with
the zero bracket. This is the Lie algebra of the connected Lie groups R and S1. (You are
asked to prove in an exercise that these are, in fact, the only connected one-dimensional
Lie groups.)
The ï¬rst interesting case, therefore, is dimension 2. If g is a 2-dimensional Lie alge-
bra with basis x1, x2, then the entire Lie algebra structure is determined by the bracket
[x1, x2] = a1x1+a2x2. If a1 = a2 = 0, then all brackets are zero, and the algebra is abelian.
If one of a1 or a2 is non-zero, then, by switching x1 and x2 if necessary, we may assume
that a1 Ì¸= 0. Then, considering the new basis y1 = a1x1 + a2x2 and y2 = (1/a1)x2, we
get [y1, y2] = y1. Since the Jacobi identity is easily veriï¬ed for this Lie bracket, this does
deï¬ne a Lie algebra. Thus, up to isomorphism, there are only two distinct 2-dimensional
Lie algebras.
The abelian example is, of course, the Lie algebra of the vector space R2 (as well as
the Lie algebra of S1 Ã— R, and the Lie algebra of S1 Ã— S1).
An example of a Lie group of dimension 2 with a non-abelian Lie algebra is the matrix
Lie group

abelian Lie group (see the Exercises).

the story becomes much more interesting. Let g be a 3-dimensional Lie algebra, and let

Lecture 3:
Group Actions on Manifolds
In this lecture, I turn from the abstract study of Lie groups to their realizations as
â€œtransformation groups.â€
Lie group actions.
Deï¬nition 1: If (G, Âµ) is a Lie group and M is a smooth manifold, then a left action of
G on M is a smooth mapping Î»: G Ã— M â†’M which satisï¬es Î»(e, m) = m for all m âˆˆM
and
Î»(Âµ(a, b), m) = Î»(a, Î»(b, m)).
Similarly, a right action of G on M is a smooth mapping Ï: M Ã— G â†’M, which satisï¬es
Ï(m, e) = m for all m âˆˆM and
Ï(m, Âµ(a, b)) = Ï(Ï(m, a), b).
For notational sanity, whenever the action (left or right) can be easily inferred from
context, we will usually write a Â· m instead of Î»(a, m) or m Â· a instead of Ï(m, a). Thus,
for example, the axioms for a left action in this abbreviated notation are simply e Â· m = m
and a Â· (b Â· m) = ab Â· m.
For a given a left action Î»: G Ã— M â†’M, it is easy to see that for each ï¬xed a âˆˆG
the map Î»a: M â†’M deï¬ned by Î»a(m) = Î»(a, m) is a smooth diï¬€eomorphism of M onto
itself. Thus, G gets represented as a group of diï¬€eomorphisms, or â€œtransformationsâ€ of a
manifold M. This notion of â€œtransformation groupâ€ was what motivated Lie to develop his
theory in the ï¬rst place. See the Appendix to this Lecture for a more complete discussion
of this point.
Equivalence of Left and Right Actions. Note that every right action Ï: M Ã—G â†’
M can be rewritten as a left action and vice versa. One merely deï¬nes
ËœÏ(a, m) = Ï(m, aâˆ’1).
(The reader should check that this ËœÏ is, in fact, a left action.) Thus, all theorems about
left actions have analogues for right actions. The distinction between the two is mainly
for notational and conceptual convenience.
I will concentrate on left actions and only
occasionally point out the places where right actions behave slightly diï¬€erently (mainly
changes of sign, etc.).
Stabilizers and Orbits. A left action is said to be eï¬€ective if g Â· m = m for all
m âˆˆM implies that g = e. (Sometimes, the word faithful is used instead.) A left action
is said to be free if g Ì¸= e implies that g Â· m Ì¸= m for all m âˆˆM.
A left action is said to be transitive if, for any x, y âˆˆM, there exists a g âˆˆG so that
g Â· x = y. In this case, M is usually said to be homogeneous under the given action.

and the stabilizer (or isotropy group) of m is deï¬ned to be the subset
Gm = {g âˆˆG | g Â· m = m}.

The following theorem says that orbits and stabilizers are particularly nice objects.
Though the proof is relatively straightforward, it is a little long, so we will consider a few
examples before attempting it.
Theorem 1:
Let Î»: G Ã— M â†’M be a left action of G on M. Then, for all m âˆˆM, the
stabilizer Gm is a closed Lie subgroup of G. Moreover, the orbit G Â· m can be given the

by Ï†(g) = Î»(g, m) is a smooth submersion.
Example 1.

Example. Suppose that G is connected and simply connected. Let g be its Lie algebra
and let [g, g] âŠ‚g be the linear subspace generated by all brackets of the form [x, y] where
x and y lie in g. Then, by the Exercises of Lecture 2, we know that [g, g] is an ideal in g
(called the commutator ideal of g). Moreover, the quotient algebra t = g/[g, g] is abelian.
Since G is connected and simply connected, Theorem 3 from Lecture 2 implies that
there is a Lie group homomorphism Ï†0: G â†’T0 = t whose induced Lie algebra homo-
morphism Ï•0: g â†’t = g/[g, g] is just the canonical quotient mapping. From our previous
remarks, it follows that any Lie equation for G can be reduced, by one quadrature, to a
Lie equation for G1 = ker Ï†0. It is not diï¬ƒcult to check that the group G1 constructed in
this argument is also connected and simply connected.
L.3.13
50

The desire to iterate this process leads to the following construction: Deï¬ne the
sequence {gk} of commutator ideals of g by the rules g0 = g and and gk+1 = [gk, gk] for
k â‰¥0. Then we have the following result:
Proposition 4:
Let G be a connected and simply connected Lie group for which the
sequence {gk} of commutator ideals satisï¬es gN = (0) for some N > 0. Then any Lie
equation for G can be solved by a sequence of quadratures.
A Lie algebra with the property described in Proposition 4 is called â€œsolvableâ€. For
example, the subalgebra of upper triangular matrices in gl(n, R) is solvable, as the reader
is invited to check.
While it may seem that solvability is a lot to ask of a Lie algebra, it turns out that
this property is surprisingly common. The reader can also check that, of all of the two
and three dimensional Lie algebras found in Lecture 2, only sl(2, R) and so(3) fail to be
solvable.
This (partly) explains why the Riccati equation holds such an important place in
the theory of ODE. In some sense, it is the ï¬rst Lie equation which cannot be solved by
quadratures. (See the exercises for an interpretation and â€œproofâ€ of this statement.)
In any case, the sequence of subalgebras {gk} eventually stabilizes at a subalgebra
gN whose Lie algebra satisï¬es [gN, gN] = gN. A Lie algebra g for which [g, g] = g is
called â€œperfectâ€. Our analysis of Lie equations shows that, by Lieâ€™s reduction method,
we can, by quadrature alone, reduce the problem of solving Lie equations to the problem
of solving Lie equations associated to Lie groups with perfect algebras. Further analysis
of the relation between the structure of a Lie algebra and the solvability by quadratures
of any associated Lie equation leads to the development of the so-called Jordan-HÂ¨older
decomposition theorems, see [?].

Appendix: Lieâ€™s Transformation Groups, I
When Lie began his study of symmetry groups in the nineteenth century, the modern
concepts of manifold theory were not available. Thus, the examples that he had to guide
him were deï¬ned as â€œtransformations in n variablesâ€ which were often, like the MÂ¨obius
transformations on the line or like conformal transformations in space, only deï¬ned â€œalmost
everywhereâ€. Thus, at ï¬rst glance, it might appear that Lieâ€™s concept of a â€œcontinuous
transformation groupâ€ should correspond to what we have deï¬ned as a local Lie group
action.
However, it turns out that Lie had in mind a much more general concept. For Lie, a
set Î“ of local diï¬€eomorphisms in Rn formed a â€œcontinuous transformation groupâ€ if it was
closed under composition and inverse and moreover, the elements of Î“ were characterized
as the solutions of some system of diï¬€erential equations.
For example, the MÂ¨obius group on the line could be characterized as the set Î“ of
(non-constant) solutions f(x) of the diï¬€erential equation
2fâ€²â€²â€²(x)fâ€²(x) âˆ’3

fâ€²â€²(x)
2 = 0.
As another example, the â€œgroupâ€ of area preserving transformations of the plane could be
characterized as the set of solutions

f(x, y), g(x, y)

to the equation
fx gy âˆ’gx fy â‰¡1,
while the â€œgroupâ€ of holomorphic transformations of the plane
R2
(regarded as C) was the set of solutions

f(x, y), g(x, y)

to the equations
fx âˆ’gy = fy + gx = 0.
Notice a big diï¬€erence between the ï¬rst example and the other two.
In the ï¬rst
example, there is only a 3-parameter family of local solutions and each of these solutions
patches together on RP1 = R âˆª{âˆ} to become an element of the global Lie group action
of SL(2, R) on RP1. In the other two examples, there are many local solutions that cannot
be extended to the entire plane, much less any â€œcompletionâ€. Moreover in the volume
preserving example, it is clear that no ï¬nite dimensional Lie group could ever contain all
of the globally deï¬ned volume preserving transformations of the plane.
Lie regarded these latter two examples as â€œinï¬nite continuous groupsâ€. Nowadays, we
would call them â€œinï¬nite dimensional pseudo-groupsâ€. I will say more about this point of
view in an appendix to Lecture 6.
Since Lie did not have a group manifold to work with, he did not regard his â€œinï¬nite
groupsâ€ as pathological. Instead of trying to ï¬nd a global description of the groups, he
worked with what he called the â€œinï¬nitesimal transformationsâ€ of Î“. We would say that,
L.3.15
52

for each of his groups Î“, he considered the space of vector ï¬elds Î³ âŠ‚X(Rn) whose (local)
ï¬‚ows were 1-parameter â€œsubgroupsâ€ of Î“. For example, the inï¬nitesimal transformations
associated to the area preserving transformations are the vector ï¬elds
X = f(x, y) âˆ‚
âˆ‚x + g(x, y) âˆ‚
âˆ‚y
which are divergence free, i.e., satisfy fx + gy = 0.
Lie â€œshowedâ€ that for any â€œcontinuous transformation groupâ€ Î“, the associated set
of vector ï¬elds Î³ was actually closed under addition, scalar multiplication (by constants),
and, most signiï¬cantly, the Lie bracket. (The reason for the quotes around â€œshowedâ€ is
that Lie was not careful to specify the nature of the diï¬€erential equations which he was
using to deï¬ne his groups. Without adding some sort of constant rank or non-degeneracy
hypotheses, many of his proofs are incorrect.)
For Lie, every subalgebra L of the algebra X(Rn) which could be characterized by
some system of pde was to be regarded the Lie algebra of some Lie group. Thus, rather
than classify actual groups (which might not really be groups because of domain problems),
Lie classiï¬ed subalgebras of the algebra of vector ï¬elds.
In the case that L was ï¬nite dimensional, Lie actually proved that there was a â€œgermâ€
of a Lie group (in our sense) and a local Lie group action which generated this algebra of
vector ï¬elds. This is Lieâ€™s so-called Third Fundamental Theorem.
The case where L was inï¬nite dimensional remained rather intractable. I will have
more to say about this in Lecture 6. For now, though, I want to stress that there is a sort
of analogue of actions for these â€œinï¬nite dimensional Lie groupsâ€.
For example, if M is a manifold and Diff(M) is the group of (global) diï¬€eomorphisms,
then we can regard the natural (evaluation) map Î»: Diff(M)Ã—M â†’M given by Î»(Ï†, m) =
Ï†(m) as a faithful Lie group action. If M is compact, then every vector ï¬eld is complete, so,
at least formally, the induced map Î»âˆ—: TidDiff(M) â†’X(M) ought to be an isomorphism
of vector spaces. If our analogy with the ï¬nite dimensional case is to hold up, Î»âˆ—must
reverse the Lie bracket.
Of course, since we have not deï¬ned a smooth structure on Diff(M), it is not im-
mediately clear how to make sense of TidDiff(M). I will prefer to proceed formally and
simply deï¬ne the Lie algebra diff(M) of Diff(M) to be the vector space X(M) with the
Lie algebra bracket given by the negative of the vector ï¬eld Lie bracket.
With this deï¬nition, it follows that a left action Î»: G Ã— M â†’M where G is ï¬nite
dimensional can simply be regarded as a homomorphism Î›: G â†’Diff(M) inducing a
homomorphism of Lie algebras.
A modern treatment of this subject can be found in [SS].

I leave it to the reader to show that HA(p Â· g) = gâˆ’1HA(p)g and that, if p and pâ€² can
be joined by a horizontal curve in P, then HA(p) = HA(pâ€²). Thus, the conjugacy class of
HA(p) in G is independent of p if M is connected.
A basic theorem due to Borel and Lichnerowitz (see [KN]) asserts that HA(p) is always
a Lie subgroup of G.

Lecture 4:

Variational Problems. In this Lecture, I will introduce a particular set of variational
problems, the so-called â€œï¬rst-order particle Lagrangian problemsâ€, which will serve as a
link to the â€œsymplecticâ€ geometry to be developed in the next Lecture.
Deï¬nition 1: A Lagrangian on a manifold M is a smooth function L: TM â†’R. For any

(The use of the word â€œfunctionalâ€ here is classical. The reader is supposed to think
of the set of all smooth curves Î³: [a, b] â†’M as a sort of inï¬nite dimensional manifold and
of FL as a function on it.)
I have deliberately chosen to avoid the (mild) complications caused by allowing less
smoothness for L and Î³, though for some purposes, it is essential to do so. The geometric
points that I want to make, however will be clearest if we do not have to worry about
determining the optimum regularity assumptions.
Also, some sources only require L to be deï¬ned on some open set in TM. Others

go into any of these (slight) extensions, the reader should be aware that they exist. For
example, see [A].
Example: Suppose that L: TM â†’R restricts to each TxM to be a positive deï¬nite

For a Lagrangian L, one is usually interested in ï¬nding the curves Î³: [a, b] â†’M with

The principle

is so fundamental that whenever a new system of equations is encountered an enormous
eï¬€ort is expended to determine its symmetries. Moreover, the intuition is often expressed
that â€œevery conservation law ought to come from some symmetryâ€, so whenever conserved
quantities are observed in Nature (or, more accurately, our models of Nature) people
nowadays look for a symmetry to explain it. Even when no symmetry is readily apparent,
in many cases a sort of â€œhidden symmetryâ€ can be found.

Deï¬nition 4:
A Lagrangian L is said to be non-degenerate if, relative to every local

is a Riemannian metric, L is non-degenerate.
Although Deï¬nition 4 is fairly explicit, it is certainly not coordinate free. Here is a
result which may clarify the meaning of non-degenerate.

Lecture 5:
Symplectic Manifolds, I
In Lecture 4, I associated a non-degenerate 2-form dÏ‰L on TM to every non-degenerate
Lagrangian L: TM â†’R. In this section, I want to begin a more systematic study of the
geometry of manifolds on which there is speciï¬ed a closed, non-degenerate 2-form.
Symplectic Algebra.
First, I will develop the algebraic precursors of the manifold concepts which are to
follow. For simplicity, all of these constructions will be carried out on vector spaces over
the reals, but they could equally well have been carried out over any ï¬eld of characteristic
not equal to 2.
Symplectic Vector Spaces. A bilinear pairing B: V Ã— V â†’R is said to be skew-
symmetric (or alternating) if B(x, y) = âˆ’B(y, x) for all x, y in V . The space of skew-
symmetric bilinear pairings on V will be denoted by A2(V ).
The set A2(V ) is a vec-
tor space under the obvious addition and scalar multiplication and is naturally identiï¬ed
with Î›2(V âˆ—), the space of exterior 2-forms on V . The elements of A2(V ) are often called
skew-symmetric bilinear forms on V. A pairing B âˆˆA2(V ) is said to be non-degenerate if,
for every non-zero v âˆˆV , there is a w âˆˆV for which B(v, w) Ì¸= 0.
Deï¬nition 1:
A symplectic space is a pair (V, B) where V is a vector space and B is a
non-degenerate, skew-symmetric, bilinear pairing on V .

Symplectic Reduction of Vector Spaces. If B: V Ã— V â†’R is a skew-symmetric
bilinear form which is not necessarily non-degenerate, then we deï¬ne the null space of B

B(x, y) = B(x, y)
where x and y are the cosets in V of x and y in V . It is easy to see that (V , B) is a
symplectic space.
Deï¬nition 2:
If B is a skew-symmetric bilinear form on a vector space V , then the
symplectic space (V , B) is called the symplectic reduction of (V, B).

Symplectic Manifolds.
We are now ready to return to the study of manifolds.
Deï¬nition 3: A symplectic structure on a smooth manifold M is a non-degenerate, closed

Symplectic Submanifolds. Let (M2m, â„¦) be a symplectic manifold. Suppose that

It is not obvious just how to ï¬nd symplectic submanifolds of M. Even though being
a symplectic submanifold is an â€œopenâ€ condition on submanifolds of M, is is not â€œdenseâ€.
One cannot hope to perturb an arbitrary even dimensional submanifold of M slightly so

of M on which a symplectic form restricts to be non-degenerate.
For example, no symplectic submanifold of R2n (with any symplectic structure on
R2n) could be compact for the following simple reason: Since R2n is contractible, its
second deRham cohomology group vanishes.

Involution
I now want to make some remarks about the meaning of the Poisson bracket and its
applications.
Deï¬nition 5:
Let (M, â„¦) be a symplectic manifold. Two functions f and g are said to
be in involution (with respect to â„¦) if they satisfy the condition {f, g} = 0.
â–¶
Note that, since {f, g} = dg(Xf) = âˆ’df(Xg), it follows that two functions f and g are
in involution if and only if each is constant on the integral curves of the otherâ€™s Hamiltonian
vector ï¬eld.
Now, if one is trying to describe the integral curves of a Hamiltonian vector ï¬eld,
Xf, the more independent functions on M that one can ï¬nd which are constant on the
integral curves of Xf, the more accurately one can describe those integral curves. If one
were able ï¬nd, in addition to f itself, 2nâˆ’2 additional independent functions on M which
are constant on the integral curves of Xf, then one could describe the integral curves of
Xf implicitly by setting those functions equal to a constant.
It turns out, however, that this is too much to hope for in general. It can happen that
a Hamiltonian vector ï¬eld Xf has no functions in involution with it except for functions
of the form F(f).

91

Nevertheless, in many cases which arise in practice, we can ï¬nd several functions in
involution with a given function f = f1 and, moreover, in involution with each other.

theorem of Liouville which says that the remaining nâˆ’1 required functions can be found
(at least locally) by quadrature alone. In the classical language, a vector ï¬eld Xf for which
such functions are known is said to be â€œcompletely integrable by quadraturesâ€, or, more
simply as â€œcompletely integrableâ€.

Lecture 6:
Symplectic Manifolds, II
The Space of Symplectic Structures on M.
I want to turn now to the problem of describing the symplectic structures a manifold M
can have. This is a surprisingly delicate problem and is currently a subject of research.
Of course, one fundamental question is whether a given manifold has any symplec-
tic structures at all.
I want to begin this lecture with a discussion of the two known
obstructions for a manifold to have a symplectic structure.
The cohomology ring condition. If â„¦âˆˆA2(M2n) is a symplectic structure on a
compact manifold M, then the cohomology class [â„¦] âˆˆH2
dR(M, R) is non-zero. In fact,
[â„¦]n = [â„¦n], but the class [â„¦n] cannot vanish in H2n
dR(M) because the integral of â„¦n over
M is clearly non-zero. Thus, we have
Proposition 1:
If M2n is compact and has a symplectic structure, there must exist an
element u âˆˆH2(M, R) so that un Ì¸= 0 âˆˆH2n
dR(M).
Example. This immediately rules out the existence of a symplectic structure on S2n for
all n > 1. One consequence of this, as you are asked to show in the Exercises, is that there
cannot be any simple notion of connected sum in the category of symplectic manifolds
(except in dimension 2).
The bundle obstruction. If M admits a symplectic structure â„¦, then, in particu-
lar, this deï¬nes a symplectic structure on each of the tangent spaces TmM which varies
continuously with m. In other words, TM must carry the structure of a symplectic vector
bundle. There are topological obstructions to the existence of such a structure on the tan-
gent bundle of a general manifold. As a simple example, if M has a symplectic structure,
then TM must be orientable.
There are more subtle obstructions than orientation. Unfortunately, a description of
these obstructions requires some acquaintance with the theory of characteristic classes.
However, part of the following discussion will be useful even to those who arenâ€™t familiar
with characteristic class theory, so I will give it now, even though the concepts will only
reveal their importance in later Lectures.
Deï¬nition 1:
An almost symplectic structure on a manifold M2n is a smooth 2-form
â„¦deï¬ned on M which is non-degenerate but not necessarily closed. An almost complex
structure on M2n is a smooth bundle map J: TM â†’TM which satisï¬es J 2v = âˆ’v for all
v in TM.
The reason that I have introduced both of these concepts at the same time is that
they are intimately related. The really deep aspects of this relationship will only become
apparent in the Lecture 9, but we can, at least, give the following result now.
L.6.1
100

Proposition 2: A manifold M2n has an almost symplectic structure if and only if it has
an almost complex structure.
Proof:
First, suppose that M has an almost complex structure J. Let g0 be any Rie-
mannian metric on M. (Thus, g0: TM â†’R is a smooth function which restricts to each
TmM to be a positive deï¬nite quadratic form.) Now deï¬ne a new Riemannian metric by
the formula
g(v) = g0(v) + g0(Jv).
Then g has the property that g(Jv) = g(v) for all v âˆˆTM since
g(Jv) = g0(Jv) + g0(J 2v) = g0(Jv) + g0(âˆ’v) = g(v).
Now let âŸ¨, âŸ©denote the (symmetric) inner product associated with g. Thus, âŸ¨v, vâŸ©=
g(v), so we have âŸ¨Jx, JyâŸ©= âŸ¨x, yâŸ©when x and y are tangent vector with the same base
point. For x, y âˆˆTmM deï¬ne â„¦(x, y) = âŸ¨Jx, yâŸ©. I claim that â„¦is a non-degenerate 2-form
on M. To see this, ï¬rst note that
â„¦(x, y) = âŸ¨Jx, yâŸ©= âˆ’âŸ¨Jx, J 2yâŸ©= âˆ’âŸ¨J 2y, JxâŸ©= âˆ’âŸ¨Jy, xâŸ©= âˆ’â„¦(y, x),
so â„¦is a 2-form. Moreover, if x is a non-zero tangent vector, then â„¦(x, Jx) = âŸ¨Jx, JxâŸ©=
g(x) > 0, so it follows that x
â„¦Ì¸= 0. Thus â„¦is non-degenerate.
To go the other way is a little more delicate.
Suppose that â„¦is given and ï¬x a
Riemannian metric g on M with associated inner product âŸ¨, âŸ©. Then, by linear algebra
there exists a unique bundle mapping A: TM â†’TM so that â„¦(x, y) = âŸ¨Ax, yâŸ©. Since â„¦is
skew-symmetric and non-degenerate, it follows that A must be skew-symmetric relative to
âŸ¨, âŸ©and must be invertible. It follows that âˆ’A2 must be symmetric and positive deï¬nite
relative to âŸ¨, âŸ©.
Now, standard results from linear algebra imply that there is a unique smooth bundle
map B: TM â†’TM which positive deï¬nite and symmetric with respect to âŸ¨, âŸ©and which
satisï¬es B2 = âˆ’A2. Moreover, this linear mapping B must commute with A. (See the
Exercises if you are not familiar with this fact). Thus, the mapping J = ABâˆ’1 satisï¬es
J 2 = âˆ’I, as desired.
â–¡
It is not hard to show that the mappings (J, g0) â†’â„¦and (â„¦, g) â†’J constructed in the
proof of Proposition 1 depend continuously (in fact, smoothly) on their arguments. Since
the set of Riemannian metrics on M is contractible, it follows that the set of homotopy
classes of almost complex structures on M is in natural one-to-one correspondence with
the set of homotopy classes of almost symplectic structures.
(The reader who is familiar with the theory of principal bundles knows that at the
heart of Proposition 1 is the fact that Sp(n, R) and GL(n, C) have the same maximal
compact subgroup, namely U(n).)

The actual conditions for a manifold to have an almost symplectic structure can be
expressed in terms of characteristic classes, so, in principle, this can always be determined
once the manifold is given explicitly. In Lecture 9 we will describe more fully the following
remarkable result of Gromov:
â–¶
If M2n has no compact components and has an almost symplectic structure Î¥, then
there exists a symplectic structure â„¦on M which is homotopic to Î¥ through almost
symplectic structures.
Thus, the problem of determining which manifolds have symplectic structures is now
reduced to the compact case. In this case, no obstruction beyond what I have already
described is known. Thus, I can state the following:
Basic Open Problem: If a compact manifold M2n satisï¬es the cohomology ring condi-
tion and has an almost symplectic structure, does it have a symplectic structure?
Even (perhaps especially) for 4-manifolds, this problem is extremely interesting and
very poorly understood.

Appendix: Lieâ€™s Transformation Groups, II
The reader who is learning symplectic geometry for the ï¬rst time may be astonished by
the richness of the subject and, at the same time, be wondering â€œAre there other geometries
like symplectic geometry which remain to be explored?â€ The point of this appendix is to
give one possible answer to this very vague question.
When Lie began his study of transformation groups in n variables, he modeled his
attack on the known study of the ï¬nite groups. Thus, his idea was that he would ï¬nd all
of the â€œsimple groupsâ€ ï¬rst and then assemble them (by solving the extension problem)
to classify the general group. Thus, if one â€œgroupâ€ G had a homomorphism onto another
â€œgroupâ€ H
1 âˆ’â†’K âˆ’â†’G âˆ’â†’H âˆ’â†’1
then one could regard G as a semi-direct product of H with the kernel subgroup K.
Guided by this idea, Lie decided that the ï¬rst task was to classify the transitive
transformation groups G, i.e., the ones which acted transitively on Rn (at least locally).
The reason for this was that, if G had an orbit S of dimension 0 < k < n, then the
restriction of the action of G to S would give a non-trivial homomorphism of G into a
transformation group in fewer variables.
Second, Lie decided that he needed to classify ï¬rst the â€œgroupsâ€ which, in his language,
â€œdid not preserve any subset of the variables.â€ The example he had in mind was the group
of diï¬€eomorphisms of R2 of the form
Ï†(x, y) =

f(x), g(x, y)

.
Clearly the assignment Ï† â†’f provides a homomorphism of this group into the group of
diï¬€eomorphisms in one variable. Lie called groups which â€œdid not preserve any subset of
the variablesâ€ primitive. In modern language, primitive is taken to mean that G does not
preserve any foliation on Rn (coordinates on the leaf space would furnish a â€œproper subset
of the variablesâ€ which was preserved by G).
Thus, the fundamental problem was to classify the â€œprimitive transitive continuous
transformation groupsâ€.
When the algebra of inï¬nitesimal generators of G was ï¬nite dimensional, Lie and his
coworkers made good progress. Their work culminated in the work of Cartan and Killing,
classifying the ï¬nite dimensional simple Lie groups. (Interestingly enough, they did not
then go on to solve the extension problem and so classify all Lie groups. Perhaps they
regarded this as a problem of lesser order. Or, more likely, the classiï¬cation turned out to
be messy, uninteresting, and ultimately intractable.)
They found that the simple groups fell into two types.
Besides the special linear
groups, such as SL(n, R), SL(n, C) and other complex analogs; orthogonal groups, such as
SO(p, q) and its complex analogs; and symplectic groups, such as Sp(n, R) and its complex
analogs (which became known as the classical groups), there were ï¬ve â€œexceptionalâ€ types.
This story is quite long, but very interesting. The â€œï¬nite dimensional Lie groupsâ€ went on
L.6.10
109

to become an essential part of the foundation of modern diï¬€erential geometry. A complete
account of this classiï¬cation (along with very interesting historical notes) can be found in
[He].
However, when the algebra of inï¬nitesimal generators of G was inï¬nite dimensional,
the story was not so complete. Lie himself identiï¬ed four classes of these â€œinï¬nite dimen-
sional primitive transitive transformation groupsâ€. They were
â€¢ In every dimension n, the full diï¬€eomorphism group, Diff(Rn).
â€¢ In every dimension n, the group of diï¬€eomorphisms which preserve a ï¬xed volume
form Âµ, denoted by SDiff(Âµ).
â€¢ In every even dimension 2n, the group of diï¬€eomorphisms which preserve the standard
symplectic form
â„¦n = dx1 âˆ§dy1 + Â· Â· Â· + dxn âˆ§dyn,
denoted by Sp(â„¦n).
â€¢ In every odd dimension 2n + 1, the group of diï¬€eomorphisms which preserve, up to a
scalar function multiple, the 1-form
Ï‰n = dz + x1 dy1 + Â· Â· Â· + xn dyn.
This â€œgroupâ€ was known as the contact group and I will denote it by Ct(Ï‰n).
However, Lie and his coworkers were never able to discover any others, though they
searched diligently. (By the way, Lie was aware that there were also holomorphic analogs
acting in Cn, but, at that time, the distinction between real and complex was not generally
made explicit. Apparently, an educated reader was supposed to know or be able to guess
what the generalizations to the complex category were.)
In a series of four papers spanning from 1902 to 1910, Â´Elie Cartan reformulated Lieâ€™s
problem in terms of systems of partial diï¬€erential equations and, under the hypothesis
of analyticity (real and complex were not carefully distinguished), he proved that Lieâ€™s
classes were essentially all of the inï¬nite dimensional primitive transitive transformation
groups. The slight extension was that SDiff(Âµ) had a companion extension to RÂ·SDiff(Âµ),
the diï¬€eomorphisms which preserve Âµ up to a constant multiple and that Sp(â„¦n) had
a companion extension to R Â· Sp(â„¦n), the diï¬€eomorphisms which preserve â„¦n up to a
constant multiple. Of course, there were also the holomorphic analogues of these. Notice
the remarkable fact that there are no â€œexceptional inï¬nite dimensional primitive transitive
transformation groupsâ€.
These papers are remarkable, not only for their results, but for the wealth of concepts
which Cartan introduced in order to solve his problem. In these papers, Cartan introduces
the notion of G-structures (of all orders), principal bundles and their connections, jet
bundles, prolongation (both of group actions and exterior diï¬€erential systems), and a host
of other ideas which were only appreciated much later. Perhaps because of its originality,
Cartanâ€™s work in this area was essentially ignored for many years.
L.6.11
110

In the 1950â€™s, when algebraic varieties were being explored and developed as complex
manifolds, it began to be understood that complex manifolds were to be thought of as
manifolds with an atlas of coordinate charts whose â€œoverlapsâ€ were holomorphic. Gener-
alizing this example, it became clear that, for any collection Î“ of local diï¬€eomorphisms of
Rn which satisï¬ed the following deï¬nition, one could deï¬ne a category of Î“-manifolds as
manifolds endowed with an atlas A of coordinate charts whose overlaps lay in A.
Deï¬nition 3:
A local diï¬€eomorphism of Rn is a pair (U, Ï†) where U âŠ‚Rn is an open
set and Ï†: U â†’Rn is a one-to-one diï¬€eomorphism onto its image.
A set Î“ of local
diï¬€eomorphisms of Rn is said to form a pseudo-group on Rn if it satisï¬es the following
three properties:
(1) (Composition and Inverses) If (U, Ï†) and (V, Ïˆ) are in Î“, then (Ï†âˆ’1(V ), Ïˆ â—¦Ï†) and
(Ï†(U), Ï†âˆ’1) also belong to Î“.
(2) (Localization and Globalization) If (U, Ï†) is in Î“, and W âŠ‚U is open, then (W, Ï†|W )
is also in Î“. Moreover, if (U, Ï†) is a local diï¬€eomorphism of Rn such that U can be
written as the union of open subsets WÎ± for which (WÎ±, Ï†|WÎ±) is in Î“ for all Î±, then
(U, Ï†) is in Î“.
(3) (Non-triviality) (Rn, id) is in Î“.
As it turned out, the pseudo-groups Î“ of interest in geometry were exactly the ones
which could be characterized as the (local) solutions of a system of partial diï¬€erential
equations, i.e., they were Lieâ€™s transformation groups. This caused a revival of interest
in Cartanâ€™s work. Consequently, much of Cartanâ€™s work has now been redone in modern
language. In particular, Cartanâ€™s classiï¬cation was redone according to modern standards
of rigor and a very readable account of this theory can be found in [SS].
In any case, symplectic geometry, seen in this light, is one of a small handful of
â€œnaturalâ€ geometries that one can impose on manifolds.

Lecture 7:

It is worth pausing to consider why this mapping is called the momentum mapping.
The reader should calculate this mapping in the case of a free particle or a rigid body

Reduction. I now want to discuss a method of taking quotients by group actions in
the symplectic category. Now, when a Lie group G acts symplectically on the left on a
symplectic manifold M, it is not generally true that the space of orbits G\M can be given
a symplectic structure, even when this orbit space can be given the structure of a smooth
manifold (for example, the quotient need not be even dimensional).
However, when the action is Poisson, there is a natural method of breaking the orbit
space G\M into a union of symplectic submanifolds provided that certain regularity criteria
are met. The procedure I will describe is known as symplectic reduction. It is due, in its
modern form, to Marsden and Weinstein (see [GS 2]).
The idea is simple: If Âµ: M â†’gâˆ—is the momentum mapping, then the G-equivariance
of Âµ implies that there is a well-deï¬ned set map
Â¯Âµ: G\M â†’G\gâˆ—.

The theorem we are about to prove asserts that, provided certain regularity criteria are
met, the subsets MÎ¾ = Â¯Âµâˆ’1(Â¯Î¾) âŠ‚G\M are symplectic manifolds in a natural way.

3. Lie Algebra Cohomology. The purpose of this exercise is to acquaint the reader

Remark: This characterization of homogeneous symplectic spaces is sometimes mis-

the cohomology groups, sometimes compactness is assumed, either for M or G. The ex-

positive entries on the diagonal. Then SL(2, R)/H has an SL(2, R)-invariant area form,
but it double covers the associated coadjoint orbit.

Lecture 8:
Recent Applications of Reduction
In this Lecture, we will see some examples of symplectic reduction and its generaliza-
tions in somewhat non-classical settings.
In many cases, we will be concerned with extra structure on M that can be carried
along in the reduction process to produce extra structure on MÎ¾. Often this extra structure
takes the form of a Riemannian metric with special holonomy, so we begin with a short
review of this topic.
Riemannian Holonomy. Let Mn be a connected and simply connected n-manifold,
and let g be a Riemannian metric on M. Associated to g is the notion of parallel transport
along curves. Thus, for each (piecewise C1) curve Î³: [0, 1] â†’M, there is associated a linear

Â´Elie Cartan was the ï¬rst to deï¬ne and study Hx. He called it the holonomy of g at x.
He assumed that Hx was always a closed Lie subgroup of SO(TxM), a result that was only
later proved by Borel and Lichnerowitz (see [KN]).
Georges de Rham, a student of Cartan, proved that, if there is a splitting TxM =

locally a product metric in the following sense: The metric g can be written as a sum of the
form g = g1 + g2 in such a way that, for every point y âˆˆM there exists a neighborhood U
of y, a coordinate chart (x1, x2): U â†’Rd1 Ã—Rd2, and metrics Â¯gi on Rdi so that gi = xâˆ—

From this discussion it follows that, in order to know which subgroups of SO(n) can

A few words of explanation and comment about Theorem 1 are in order.
First, a Riemannian symmetric space is a Riemannian manifold diï¬€eomorphic to a
homogeneous space G/H where H âŠ‚G is essentially the ï¬xed subgroup of an involutory
homomorphism Ïƒ: G â†’G that is endowed with a G-invariant metric g that is also invariant
under the involution Î¹: G/H â†’G/H deï¬ned by Î¹(aH) = Ïƒ(a)H. The classiï¬cation of the
Riemannian symmetric spaces reduces to a classiï¬cation problem in the theory of Lie
algebras and was solved by Cartan.
Thus, the Riemannian symmetric spaces may be
regarded as known.
Second, among the holonomies of non-symmetric metrics listed in the table, the ranges
for n have been restricted so as to avoid repetition or triviality. Thus, U(1) = SO(2) and
SU(1) = {e} while Sp(1) = SU(2), and Sp(1)Sp(1) = SO(4).
Third, according to S. T. Yauâ€™s celebrated proof of the Calabi Conjecture, any compact
complex manifold for which the canonical bundle is trivial and that has a KÂ¨ahler metric
also has a Ricci-ï¬‚at KÂ¨ahler metric (see [Bes]). For this reason, metrics with holonomy
SU(m) are often referred to as Calabi-Yau metrics.
Finally, I will not attempt to discuss the proof of Theorem 1 in these notes. Even
with modern methods, the proof of this result is non-trivial and, in any case, would take
us far from our present interests. Instead, I will content myself with the remark that it
is now known that every one of these groups does, in fact, occur as the holonomy of a
Riemannian metric on a manifold of the appropriate dimension. I refer the reader to [Bes]
for a complete discussion.

We will be particularly interested in the KÂ¨ahler and hyperKÂ¨ahler cases since these
cases can be characterized by the condition that the holonomy of g leaves invariant certain
closed non-degenerate 2-forms.
Hence these cases represent symplectic manifolds with
â€œextra structureâ€, namely a compatible metric.
The basic result will be that, for a manifold M that carries one of these two structures,
there is a reduction process that can be applied to suitable group actions on M that preserve
the structure.
KÂ¨ahler Manifolds and Algebraic Geometry.
In this section, we give a very brief introduction to KÂ¨ahler manifolds.
These are

with great frequency in Algebraic Geometry, and it is beyond the scope of these Lectures
to do more than make an introduction to their uses here.

Almost Hermitian Manifolds. Since our main interest is in symplectic and com-
plex structures, I will introduce the notion of an almost Hermitian structure on a manifold
in terms of its almost complex and almost symplectic structures:
Deï¬nition 2:
Let M2n be a manifold. A 2-form â„¦and an almost complex structure J
deï¬ne an almost Hermitian structure on M if, for each m âˆˆM, the pair (â„¦m, Jm) deï¬nes
a Hermitian structure on TmM.
When (â„¦, J) deï¬nes an almost Hermitian structure on M, the Riemannian metric g
on M deï¬ned by g(v) = â„¦(v, Jv) is called the associated metric.
Just as one must place conditions on an almost symplectic structure in order to get a
symplectic structure, there are conditions that an almost complex structure must satisfy
in order to be a complex structure.
Deï¬nition 3: An almost complex structure J on M2n is integrable if each point of M has a
neighborhood U on which there exists a coordinate chart z: U â†’Cn so that zâ€²(Jv) = Ä± zâ€²(v)
for all v âˆˆTU. Such a coordinate chart is said to be J-holomorphic.
According to the Korn-Lichtenstein theorem, when n = 1 all almost complex struc-
tures are integrable. However, for n â‰¥2, one can easily write down examples of almost
complex structures J that are not integrable. (See the Exercises.)
When J is an integrable almost complex structure on M, the set
UJ = {(U, z) | z: U â†’Cn is J-holomorphic}
forms an atlas of charts that are holomorphic on overlaps. Thus, UJ deï¬nes a holomorphic
structure on M.
The reader may be wondering just how one determines whether an almost complex
structure is integrable or not. In the Exercises, you are asked to show that, for an integrable
almost complex structure J , the identity LJXJâˆ’Jâ—¦LXJ = 0 must hold for all vector ï¬elds
X on M. It is a remarkable result, due to Newlander and Nirenberg, that this condition
is suï¬ƒcient for J to be integrable.
â–¶
The reason that I mention this condition is that it shows that integrability is deter-
mined by J and its ï¬rst derivatives in any local coordinate system. This condition can be
rephrased as the condition that the vanishing of a certain tensor NJ, called the Nijnhuis
tensor of J and constructed out of the ï¬rst-order jet of J at each point, is necessary and
suï¬ƒcient for the integrability of J.
We are now ready to name the various integrability conditions that can be deï¬ned for
an almost Hermitian manifold.

133

Deï¬nition 4:
We call an almost Hermitian pair (â„¦, J) on a manifold M almost KÂ¨ahler
if â„¦is closed, Hermitian if J is integrable, and KÂ¨ahler if â„¦is closed and J is integrable.
We already saw in Lecture 6 that a manifold has an almost complex structure if and
only if it has an almost symplectic structure.
However, this relationship does not, in
general, hold between complex structures and symplectic structures.
Example: Here is a complex manifold that has no symplectic structure. Let Z act on
M = C2\{0} by n Â· z = 2nz. This free action preserves the standard complex structure on
M. Let N = Z\ Ëœ

manifold.

manifold satisfying H2

discussed in Lecture 6, we see that M cannot be given a symplectic structure.

The fundamental theorem relating the two â€œintegrability conditionsâ€ to the idea of
holonomy is the following one. We only give the idea of the proof because a complete proof
would require the development of considerable machinery.
Theorem 2:
An almost Hermitian structure (â„¦, J) on a manifold M is KÂ¨ahler if and
only if the form â„¦is parallel with respect to the parallel transport of the associated metric
g.
Proof: (Idea) Once the formulas are developed, it is not diï¬ƒcult to see that the covariant
derivatives of â„¦with respect to the Levi-Civita connection of g are expressible in terms of
the exterior derivative of â„¦and the Nijnhuis tensor of J. Conversely, the exterior derivative
of â„¦and the Nijnhuis tensor of J can be expressed in terms of the covariant derivative
of â„¦with respect to the Levi-Civita connection of g. Thus, â„¦is covariant constant (i.e.,
invariant under parallel translation with respect to g) if and only it is closed and J is
integrable.
â–¡
â–¶
It is worth remarking that J is invariant under parallel transport with respect to g if
and only if â„¦is.
The reason for this is that J is determined from and determines â„¦once g is ï¬xed.
The observation now follows, since g is invariant under parallel transport with respect to
its own Levi-Civita connection.

and the geometry of this KÂ¨ahler metric is the subject of current interest.

At ï¬rst glance, Deï¬nition 7 may seem surprising. After all, it appears to place no

of these structures comes for free:
Theorem 4:
For an almost hyperKÂ¨ahler structure (â„¦1, â„¦2, â„¦3) on a manifold M4n, the
following are equivalent:
(1) dâ„¦1 = dâ„¦2 = dâ„¦3 = 0.
(2) Each of the 2-forms â„¦a is parallel with respect to the Levi-Civita connection of the
associated metric.
(3) Each of the almost complex structures Ri, Rj, and Rk are integrable.
Proof: (Idea) The proof of Theorem 4 is much like the proof of Theorem 2. One shows
by local calculations in Gauss normal coordinates at any point on M that the covariant
derivatives of the forms â„¦a with respect to the Levi-Civita connection of the associated
metric can be expressed in terms of the coeï¬ƒcients of their exterior derivatives and vice-
versa. Similarly, one shows that the formulas for the Nijnhuis tensors of the three almost
complex structures on M can be expressed in terms of the covariant derivatives of the
three 2-forms and vice-versa. This is a rather formidable linear algebra problem, but it is
nothing more. I will not do the computation here.
â–¡
Note that Theorem 4 implies that the holonomy H of the associated metric of a
hyperKÂ¨ahler structure on M4n must be a subgroup of Sp(n). If H is a proper subgroup
of Sp(n), then by Theorem 1, the associated metric must be locally a product metric. Now,
as is easy to verify, the only products from Bergerâ€™s List that can appear as subgroups
of Sp(n) are products of the form

where {e}n0 âŠ‚Sp(n0) is just the identity subgroup and n = n0 + Â· Â· Â· + nk.

example with hyperKÂ¨ahler structures whose holonomy is the full Sp(ni). (If M is simply
connected and the associated metric is complete, then the de Rham Splitting Theorem
asserts that M can be globally written as a product of such metrics.) This motivates our
calling a hyperKÂ¨ahler structure on M4n irreducible if its holonomy is equal to Sp(n).
The reader may be wondering just how common these hyperKÂ¨ahler structures are

The ï¬rst known compact example was furnished by Yauâ€™s solution of the Calabi Con-
jecture:
Example: K3 Surfaces. A K3 surface is a compact simply connected 2-dimensional
complex manifold S with trivial canonical bundle. What this latter condition means is that
there is nowhere-vanishing holomorphic 2-form Î¥ on S. An example of such a surface is a
smooth algebraic surface of degree 4 in CP3.
A fundamental result of Siu [Si] is that every K3 surface is KÂ¨ahler, i.e., that there
exists a 2-form â„¦on S so that the hermitian structure (â„¦, J) on S is actually KÂ¨ahler.
Moreover, Yauâ€™s solution of the Calabi Conjecture implies that â„¦can be chosen so that Î¥
is parallel with respect to the Levi-Civita connection of the associated metric.
Multiplying Î¥ by an appropriate constant, we can arrange that 2 â„¦2 = Î¥âˆ§Î¥. Since

For a long time, the K3 surfaces were the only known compact manifolds with hy-
perKÂ¨ahler structures. In fact, a â€œproofâ€ was published showing that there were no other
compact ones. However, this turned out not to be correct.
Example: Let M4n be a simply connected, compact complex manifold (of complex
dimension 2n) with a holomorphic symplectic form Î¥. Then Î¥n is a non-vanishing holo-
morphic volume form, and hence the canonical bundle of M is trivial. If M has a KÂ¨ahler
structure that is compatible with its complex structure, then, by Yauâ€™s solution of the
Calabi Conjecture, there is a KÂ¨ahler metric g on M for which the volume form Î¥n is
parallel. This implies that the holonomy of g is a subgroup of SU(2n). However, this in
turn implies that g is Ricci-ï¬‚at and hence, by a Bochner vanishing argument, that every
holomorphic form on M is parallel with respect to g. Thus, Î¥ is also parallel with respect
to g and hence the holonomy is a subgroup of Sp(n). If M can be constructed in such a
way that it cannot be written as a non-trivial product of complex submanifolds, then the
holonomy of g must act irreducibly on C2n and hence must equal Sp(n).
Fujita was the ï¬rst to construct a simply connected, compact complex 4-manifold that
carried a holomorphic 2-form and that could not be written non-trivially as a product. This
work is written up in detail in a survey article by [Bea].

The method of hyperKÂ¨ahler reduction has a wide variety of applications. Many of the
interesting moduli spaces for Yang-Mills theory turn out to have hyperKÂ¨ahler structures
because of this reduction procedure. For example, as Atiyah and Hitchin [AH] show, the
space of magnetic monopoles of â€œchargeâ€ k on R3 turns out to have a natural hyperKÂ¨ahler
structure that is derived by methods extremely similar to the example presented earlier of
a KÂ¨ahler structure on the moduli space of ï¬‚at connections over a Riemann surface.
Peter Kronheimer [Kr] has used the method of hyperKÂ¨ahler reduction to construct,
for each quotient manifold Î£ of S3, an asymptotically locally Euclidean (ALE) Ricci-ï¬‚at
self-dual Einstein metric on a 4-manifold MÎ£ whose boundary at inï¬nity is Î£. He then
went on to prove that all such metrics on 4-manifolds arise in this way.
Finally, it should also be mentioned that the case of metrics on manifolds M4n with
holonomy Sp(n) Â· Sp(1) can also be treated by the method of reduction. I donâ€™t have time
to go into this here, but the reader can ï¬nd a complete account in [GL].

Lecture 9:
The Gromov School of Symplectic Geometry
In this lecture, I want to describe some of the remarkable new information we have
about symplectic manifolds owing to the inï¬‚uence of the ideas of Mikhail Gromov. The
basic reference for much of this material is Gromovâ€™s remarkable book Partial Diï¬€erential
Relations.
The fundamental idea of studying complex structures â€œtamed byâ€ a given symplectic
structure was developed by Gromov in a remarkable paper Pseudo-holomorphic Curves on
Almost Complex Manifolds and has proved extraordinarily fruitful. In the latter part of
this lecture, I will try to introduce the reader to this theory.
Soft Techniques in Symplectic Manifolds
Symplectic Immersions and Embeddings.
Before beginning on the topic of
symplectic immersions, let me recall how the theory of immersions in the ordinary sense
goes.
Recall that the Whitney Immersion Theorem (in the weak form) asserts that any
smooth n-manifold M has an immersion into R2n. This result is proved by ï¬rst immersing
M into some RN for N â‰«0 and then using Sardâ€™s Theorem to show that if N > 2n,
one can ï¬nd a vector u âˆˆRN so that u is not tangent to f(M) at any point. Then the
projection of f(M) onto a hyperplane orthogonal to u is still an immersion, but now into
RNâˆ’1.
This result is not the best possible. Whitney himself showed that one could always im-
merse Mn into R2nâˆ’1, although â€œgeneral positionâ€ arguments are not suï¬ƒcient to do this.
This raises the question of determining what the best possible immersion or embedding
dimension is.
One topological obstruction to immersing Mn into Rn+k can be described as follows:
If f: M â†’Rn+k is an immersion, then the trivial bundle fâˆ—(TRn+k) = M Ã— Rn+k can
be split into a direct sum fâˆ—(TRn+k) = TM âŠ•Î½f where Î½f is the normal bundle of the
immersion f. Thus, if there is no bundle Î½ of rank k over M so that TM âŠ•Î½ is trivial,
then there can be no immersion of M into Rn+k.
The remarkable fact is that this topological necessary condition is almost suï¬ƒcient. In
fact, we have the following result of Hirsch and Smale for the general immersion problem.
Theorem 1:
Let M and N be connected smooth manifolds and suppose either that M
is non-compact or else that dim(M) < dim(N). Let f: M â†’N be a continuous map, and
suppose that there is a vector bundle Î½ over M so that fâˆ—(TN) = TM âŠ•Î½. Then f is
homotopic to an immersion of M into N
Theorem 1 can be interpreted as an example of what Gromov calls the h-principle,
which I now want to describe.
L.9.1
153

The h-Principle. Let Ï€: V â†’X be a surjective submersion. A section of Ï€ is, by
deï¬nition, a map Ïƒ: X â†’V which satisï¬es Ï€ â—¦Ïƒ = idX. Let J k(X, V ) denote the space
of k-jets of sections of V , and let Ï€k: J k(X, V ) â†’X denote the basepoint or â€œsourceâ€
projection. Given any section s of Ï€, there is an associated section jk(s) of Ï€k which is
deï¬ned by letting jk(s)(x) be the k-jet of s at x âˆˆX. A section Ïƒ of Ï€k is said to be
holonomic if Ïƒ = jk(s) for some section s of Ï€.
A partial diï¬€erential relation of order k for Ï€ is a subset R âŠ‚J k(X, V ). A section s
of Ï€ is said to satisfy R if jk(s)(X) âŠ‚R. We can now make the following deï¬nition:
Deï¬nition 1: A partial diï¬€erential relation R âŠ‚J k(X, V ) satisï¬es the h-principle if, for
every section Ïƒ of Ï€k which satisï¬es Ïƒ(X) âŠ‚R, there is a one-parameter family of sections
Ïƒt (0 â‰¤t â‰¤1) of Ï€k which satisfy the conditions that Ïƒt(X) âŠ‚R for all t, that Ïƒ0 = Ïƒ,
and that Ïƒ1 is holonomic.
Very roughly speaking, a partial diï¬€erential relation satisï¬es the h-principle if, when-
ever the â€œtopologicalâ€ conditions for a solution to exist are satisï¬ed, then a solution exists.
For example, if X = M and V = M Ã— N, where dim(N) â‰¥dim(M), then there is
an (open) subset R = Imm(M, N) âŠ‚J 1(M, M Ã— N) which consists of the 1-jets of graphs
of (local) immersions of M into N. What the Hirsch-Smale immersion theory says is that
Imm(M, N) satisï¬es the h-principle if either dim M = dim N and M has no compact
component or else dim M < dim N.
Of course, the h-principle does not hold for every relation R.
The real question
is how to determine when the h-principle holds for a given R. Gromov has developed
several extremely general methods for proving that the h-principle holds for various partial
diï¬€erential relations R which arise in geometry.
These methods include his theory of
topological sheaves and techniques like his method of convex integration. They generally
work in situations where the local solutions of a given partial diï¬€erential relation R are
easy to come by and it is mainly a question of â€œpatching togetherâ€ local solutions which
are fairly â€œï¬‚exibleâ€.
Gromov calls this collection of techniques â€œsoftâ€ to distinguish them from the â€œhardâ€
techniques, such as elliptic theory, which come from analysis and deal with situations where
the local solutions are somewhat â€œrigidâ€.
Here is a sample of some of the results which Gromov obtains by these methods:
Theorem 2:

Blowing Up in the Symplectic Category. We have already seen in Lecture 6 that
certain operations on smooth manifolds cannot be carried out in the symplectic category.
For example, one cannot form connected sums in the symplectic category.

as that for the Cauchy-Riemann equations. Assuming that J is suï¬ƒciently regular (Câˆis
suï¬ƒcient and we will always have this) there are plenty of local solutions. What is at issue
is the nature of the global solutions.
A parametrized holomorphic curve in M is a holomorphic map f: Î£ â†’M. Sometimes
we will want to consider unparametrized holomorphic curves in M, namely equivalence
classes [Î£, f] of holomorphic curves in M where (Î£1, f1) is equivalent to (Î£2, f2) if there
exists a holomorphic map Ï†: Î£1 â†’Î£2 satisfying f1 = f2 â—¦Ï†.
L.9.5
157

We are going to be particularly interested in the space of holomorphic curves in M.
Here are some properties that hold in the case of holomorphic curves in actual complex
manifolds and it would be nice to know if they also hold for holomorphic curves in almost
complex manifolds.
Local Finite Dimensionality. If Î£ is a compact Riemann surface, and f: Î£ â†’M
is a holomorphic curve, it is reasonable to ask what the space of â€œnearbyâ€ holomorphic
curves looks like. Because the equations which determine these mappings are elliptic and
because Î£ is compact, it follows without too much diï¬ƒculty that the space of nearby
holomorphic curves is ï¬nite dimensional. (We do not, in general know that it is a smooth
manifold!)
Intersections. A pair of distinct complex curves in a complex surface always inter-
sect at isolated points and with positive â€œmultiplicity.â€ This follows from complex analytic
geometry. This result is extremely useful because it allows us to derive information about
actual numbers of intersection points of holomorphic curves by applying topological inter-
section formulas. (Usually, these topological intersection formulas only count the number
of signed intersections, but if the surfaces can only intersect positively, then the topological
intersection numbers (counted with multiplicity) are the actual intersection numbers.)
KÂ¨ahler Area Bounds. If M happens to be a KÂ¨ahler manifold, with KÂ¨ahler form
â„¦, then the area of the image of a holomorphic curve f: Î£ â†’M is given by the formula

In particular, since â„¦is closed, the right hand side of this equation depends only on the
homotopy class of f as a map into M. Thus, if (Î£t, ft) is a continuous one-parameter
family of closed holomorphic curves in a KÂ¨ahler manifold, then they all have the same
area. This is a powerful constraint on how the images can behave, as we shall see.
Now the ï¬rst two of these properties go through without change in the case of almost
complex manifolds.
In the case of local ï¬niteness, this is purely an elliptic theory result. Studying the
linearization of the equations at a solution will even allow one to predict, using the Atiyah-
Singer Index Theorem, an upper bound for the local dimension of the moduli space and,
in some cases, will allow us to conclude that the moduli space near a given closed curve is
actually a smooth manifold (see below).
As for pairs of complex curves in an almost complex surface, Gromov has shown that
they do indeed only intersect in isolated points and with positive multiplicity (unless they
have a common component, of course). Both Gromov and Dusa McDuï¬€have used this
fact to study the geometry of symplectic 4-manifolds.
The third property is only valid for KÂ¨ahler manifolds, but it is highly desirable. The
behaviour of holomorphic curves in compact KÂ¨ahler manifolds is well understood in a large
part because of this area bound. This motivated Gromov to investigate ways of generalizing
this property.
